Letter from the
President




                                                                                                                   11.11.2025
                  Facilities Master Plan for Sacramento City College:
                  Continuing the Tradition, Evolving for Tomorrow




                                                                                                                   SCC MASTER PLAN
                  Sacramento City College began serving students in 1916 on the top floor of Sacramento
                  High School. At the time, the institution was called Sacramento Junior College, and ten
                  years later it moved to what was then the south end of the city, its current location at
                  Sutterville and Freeport. For decades SCC was the only college between CSU Chico and
                  University of Pacific in Stockton. One can feel this history today when walking onto the
                  campus amid the mature landscaping and picturesque brick buildings.




                                                                                                                   HMC ARCHITECTS
                  Throughout its history, SCC has adapted its physical infrastructure to create the best
                  possible learning environments for students. In recent years, the college has built a new
                  Student Services building with modern workflow activities for Admission and Records,
                  Financial Aid, and other services. On its second floor, the building houses a state-of-
                  the-art photography lab. Even more recently, on the south end of campus, a new Natural
                  Sciences Building and Mohr Hall have provided up-to-date lecture and lab facilities for
                  disciplines in the sciences and for our health profession programs including nursing.
                  These buildings, beautifully cased in brick to augment the historic feel of the campus,
                  house the modern rooms and equipment students need to excel in their studies.

                  The Facilities Master Plan you are reading now addresses older buildings and outdoor
                  locations on campus, facilities that need revitalizing to meet the needs of our modern
                  students. This document has gone through a process of thorough planning with input
                  from the college community including students, classified professionals, faculty, and
                  administrators. It represents the collective vision and forward thinking of the college as
                  a whole, a vision that is squarely focused on the needs of the students, how those needs
                  have evolved over time, and how our faculty, staff, and administrators can best create
                  environments of learning and fulfillment.

                  Indeed, Sacramento City College has a rich history in Sacramento. The institution is part
                  of the fabric of the city and region. Its value is that it is directly tied to the community’s
                  educational and workforce needs. For that reason, we at the college are very conscious
                  of spending public money wisely and focusing its use on the SCC mission of serving
                  our local students in life-changing ways. This Facilities Master Plan helps to guide
                  SCC’s direction so the college will continue to be what it has been to generations of
                  Sacramentans, a focal point of higher education that changes lives for the better.




                                                                                             Dr. Albert Garcia
                                                                           President, Sacramento City College

                                                                                                                            4
District Overview
LOS RIOS COMMUNITY COLLEGE DISTRICT




                                                              11.11.2025
            The Los Rios Community College District
            (LRCCD) is one of California’s largest public
            higher education systems, serving nearly




                                                              SCC MASTER PLAN
            70,000 students in the greater Sacramento
            area. It encompasses four colleges:
            American River College, Sacramento City
            College, Cosumnes River College, and
            Folsom Lake College, with 13 total campus
            locations across Sacramento, El Dorado,
            Yolo, Placer, and Solano counties.




                                                              HMC ARCHITECTS
            The district provides accessible, affordable
            education and offers associate degrees,
            transfer programs, and over 120 career-
            focused certificates.

            Los Rios Community College District
            emphasizes student success through
            programs like Guided Pathways and the
            Los Rios Promise, which offers two years of
            free tuition for eligible first-time, full-time
            students. These efforts aim to streamline
            educational journeys and improve equity in
            access and completion.

            The district serves a diverse student
            population, with strong representation
            from Hispanic/Latino, Asian, and African
            American communities. Many students are
            low-income or the first in their families to
            attend college. Equity and inclusion are
            core values, supported through dedicated
            resources and programs.

                                                CONDITIONS
                                                EXISTING
Overview




                                                     11.11.2025
      Sacramento City College and its Centers
      include a mix of historic and modern
      facilities shaped by decades of growth.
      The campuses offer a strong base for




                                                     SCC MASTER PLAN
      instruction and student services, with
      a variety of academic, administrative,
      and community spaces. However, aging
      buildings and infrastructure, disparate site
      improvements, limited wayfinding, and
      gaps in connectivity point to the need for
      targeted and coordinated upgrades. This
      assessment outlines current strengths and




                                                     HMC ARCHITECTS
      challenges to guide future planning.




                                                          12
Development History




                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      11.11.2025
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      SCC MASTER PLAN
                               1930's                                1940's                               1950's                                1960's                                1990's                                 2025

      Sacramento City College (SCC), founded in 1916 as part of Sacramento High School, is the oldest institution of higher education in Sacramento and the seventh oldest public community college in California. Originally named




                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      HMC ARCHITECTS
      Sacramento Junior College, the college was established by Belle Cooledge and was created to offer an affordable college education and serve as a community hub. The first year of operation featured an all-female graduating
      class, demonstrating the college’s early commitment to inclusion.

      Sacramento City College expanded its reach regionally through the opening of the Davis Center at UC Davis and the West Sacramento Center in 1966 and 1999, respectively. Over the years, SCC has expanded to meet the
      evolving educational needs of the region. Today, it serves around 23,000 students annually with a broad range of programs, including transfer pathways, career training, and online learning.

HSP   HOOS SWIMMING POOL                     PAN   PANTHERS CARE              UNS   UNION STADIUM                                       Located at 3835 Freeport Boulevard, the 73-acre main campus blends historic architecture with
LRC   JOAN DIDION LEARNING RESOURCE CENTER   PAS   PARKING STRUCTURE          VEC   VEHICLE COMPOUND                                    modern facilities to serve the local community.




                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  SCC MASTER PLAN
                                                                                                                                        The campus features academic buildings arranged around courtyards and landscaped quads, creating
                                                                                                                                        opportunities for the connection of indoor and outdoor spaces.

                                                                                                                                        Situated across Freeport Boulevard from Sacramento’s William Land Park, the campus has a strong
                                                                                                                                        institutional presence that serves as a “bookend” to Sacramento’s largest urban green space.




                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  HMC ARCHITECTS
                                                                                                                                          Key campus facilities include:

                                                                                                                                          •      The recently completed Natural Sciences Building, a 54,574-square-foot, LEED Gold-certified
                                                                                                                                                 structure featuring advanced laboratories, classrooms, and collaborative spaces designed to
                                                                                                                                                 support hybrid learning and student engagement.

                                                                                                                                          •      Mohr Hall, completed in 2020, offers modern academic labs and outdoor learning areas while
                                                                                                                                                 maintaining the campus’s traditional brick and terracotta aesthetic.

                                                                                                                                          •      Hughes Stadium, a 20,311-seat venue opened in 1928 and has served as a hub for athletic events
                                                                                                                                                 and community gatherings since that time.

                                                                                                                                        Additional facilities include the Performing Arts Center, Kondos and Russ Solomon Galleries, the Hulbe
                                                                                                                                        Observatory, and a Makerspace open to students, faculty, and staff.




                                                                                           Existing Main Campus Site Plan
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                       14
West Sacramento Center
EXISTING CONDITION




                                                                                                                        11.11.2025
            Situated in the civic core of West Sacramento, the Center plays an important role in providing
            accessible higher education to the community. Its proximity to public transit and municipal services is a
            strength; however, the surrounding area lacks vibrancy and active street life, which affects the sense of




                                                                                                                        SCC MASTER PLAN
            arrival and engagement.

            Building Overview

            Year Built: 2010

            Size: ~20,000 square feet




                                                                                                                        HMC ARCHITECTS
            Facilities: General classrooms, computer labs, administrative offices, and student support services

            Condition: Structurally sound and well-maintained

            The facility is well-maintained and efficiently used, though its smaller scale limits flexibility for
            programming or future growth. Faculty workrooms are shared, and students have access to limited
            informal gathering or study spaces.

Davis Center
EXISTING CONDITION




                                                                                                                      11.11.2025
            Located on the UC Davis campus, the Davis Center serves a diverse student body and benefits from
            strong connections to both academic and community resources. Its integration within the broader UC
            Davis environment supports collaboration and visibility, and students report that the location works




                                                                                                                      SCC MASTER PLAN
            well.

            Building Overview

            Year Built: Original facility in 2012; expansion completed in 2018

            Facilities: Classrooms, student services, study areas, shared faculty workspaces, and reservable




                                                                                                                      HMC ARCHITECTS
            meeting rooms

            Condition: Modern, flexible, and well-maintained

                                                                                                                                18
Institutional Strategic Plan Highlights




                                                                                              11.11.2025
       The Los Rios Community College District (LRCCD) Institutional Strategic Plan
       defines district-wide goals, outcomes, and measurable objectives that guide
       all college and district plans. It provides the foundation for all other plans, most
       importantly the college’s Education Masterplan. The Institutional Strategic Plan




                                                                                              SCC MASTER PLAN
       highlights the following district-wide goals:


       1.   Optimize student access, progress, momentum, and success.

       2. Ensure equitable academic achievement across all racial, ethnic, socio-
          economic, and gender groups.




                                                                                              HMC ARCHITECTS
       3. Provide exemplary teaching and learning opportunities.

       4. Foster an outstanding working and learning environment.

       5. Provide exemplary workforce and Career Technical Education programs that
          reflect the needs of the region served.

       6. Create and promote a culture of environmental stewardship and
          sustainability at college




                                                                                                   19
Facilities Condition Index
MAIN CAMPUS                                                                                               LEGEND




                                                                                                                                                               11.11.2025
                                                                                                                   LOW FCI (GOOD CONDITION)


                                                                                                                   MEDIUM FCI (FAIR CONDITION)




                                                                                                                                                               SCC MASTER PLAN
                                                                                                                   HIGH FCI (POOR CONDITION)




                                                                                                                                                               HMC ARCHITECTS
          Sacramento City College participates in the California Community College Facility
          Condition Assessment Program, which provides a standardized tool for evaluating the
          condition of campus buildings and planning necessary repair work. A key metric used in
          this program is the Facility Condition Index (FCI), which compares the cost of correcting
          a facility’s deficiencies to its total replacement value. Based on the FCI, each facility is
          categorized as follows:

                                         Low FCI (Good Condition):
                                         FCI less than 30

                                         Medium FCI (Fair Condition):
                                         FCI between 31 and 60

                                         High FCI (Poor Condition):
                                         FCI greater than 61

          While most buildings were classified according to these thresholds, a few were adjusted to
          a different category based on additional factors—such as the suitability of the building for
          its current academic or programmatic use, or recent upgrades and renovations not fully
          reflected in the assessment data. These adjustments ensure that facility planning aligns
          more closely with current campus needs and program goals.

          Most buildings at Sacramento City college were built in late 30’s, 50’s and 70’s and
          several of them are in need of siginficant upgrades.

                                                                                                                                                                   20
Campus Forum




                                                                                                                    11.11.2025
     On May 6th 2025, Sacramento City College hosted an on-campus forum inviting the entire SCC
     community—including faculty, staff, and students—to participate in a collaborative dialogue centered
     on the future of the college. The forum served as a key milestone in the development of the Facilities
     Master Plan (FMP) and the articulation of a long-range vision for the campus.




                                                                                                                    SCC MASTER PLAN
     Attendees took part in a series of interactive activities and structured discussions designed to solicit
     meaningful input on current campus conditions, future needs, and aspirational goals. These activities
     provided valuable insight into the community’s priorities and expectations, ensuring the FMP reflects
     the collective vision of its stakeholders.

     The following pages present a detailed summary of the forum’s engagement process, key findings, and
     emerging ideas that will inform and shape the planning framework moving forward.




                                                                                                                    HMC ARCHITECTS
     Some key takeaways from the session include:

                                        Campus Impressions
                                        MOST FAVORITE VS. LEAST FAVORITE PLACES ON CAMPUS




                                                                                                                                                              11.11.2025
                                                     The poll results indicate that both students and faculty members generally hold a positive perception
                                                     of the campus environment. Among the most favored locations, the Learning Resource Center (LRC),
                                                     the Makerspace, and the central Quad consistently ranked at the top. The recently constructed Science




                                                                                                                                                              SCC MASTER PLAN
          MOST FAVORITE PLACE
                                                     Building and Performing Arts Center were also highly regarded, emerging as close contenders among
                                                     the most appreciated campus facilities.
          LEAST FAVORITE PLACE


          SOCIALIZE
                                                     Conversely, the North Parking Garage received the lowest favorability ratings, followed by general
                                                     parking areas and key intersection zones. Security concerns were cited as the primary reason for these
          FOCUS                                      negative perceptions. Additional areas of concern included the existing gym facilities and Rodda Hall
                                                     buildings, which drew a notable degree of dissatisfaction from respondents.




                                                                                                                                                              HMC ARCHITECTS
                                        Campus Retreats
                                        PLACES TO SOCIALIZE VS. PLACES TO FOCUS




                                                     As part of the campus forum, students, staff and faculty were invited to identify locations on campus
                                                     where they prefer to focus and where they choose to socialize. The resulting map highlights key areas
                                                     that support concentrated study as well as spaces that foster connection and community. These
                                                     insights help illustrate how the campus is currently used and can guide improvements to better support
                                                     both academic and social needs.

             Students at Sacramento City College describe the campus as grounded, calm, and historically                                                                                                                               More flexibility in taking online classes, and expanding the
             structured—reflecting its long-standing roots in the community.                                                                                                                                                           course offerings at the Davis Center

             Many appreciate the peaceful atmosphere and the sense of tradition, but there’s also a strong
             desire for change.

             Students are looking for more energy on campus—more activity, more collaboration, and more
             opportunities to connect across programs and spaces. They want the college to feel more
             contemporary and flexible, both in how learning happens and how spaces are used.
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        BETTER INFORMATION
             There’s a growing expectation for SCC to better reflect today’s student needs: more coordinated
             resources, updated environments, and a stronger sense of community at the heart of campus life.

100 Ideas
IDEAS FOR CAMPUS IMPROVEMENTS




                                                                                                              11.11.2025
           Participants were asked to list their top priorities for campus improvements. The most
           frequently cited issues, ranked in order of response frequency, were as follows




                                                                                                              SCC MASTER PLAN
                            SAFETY & SECURITY
                            •   Students noted a perceived lack of safety and security on campus,
                                particularly after dark, due to inconsistent lighting and the presence of
                                dark corners and potential hiding places in the landscape and buildings.




                                                                                                              HMC ARCHITECTS
                            •   Multiple students expressed a perceived lack of security in the main
                                parking garage during all hours of the day, but especially during evening
                                hours.




                            INSTRUCTIONAL SPACES &
                            FACILITIES IMPROVEMENT
                            Students and faculty emphasized the urgent need to improve facilities—
                            especially bathrooms and drinking fountains in Rodda Hall North and South as
                            well as the gym buildings, hallways and classrooms in both Rodda Halls, and
                            the entire Business building




                            TRANSPARENCY
                            Many respondents expressed a need for greater transparency around campus
                            events, security, and safety.




                            PARKING
                            Campus users expressed dissatisfaction with the current parking
                            configuration, noting that the walk from the north parking lot to the buildings
                            at the southeast corner of campus (i.e., Natural Sciences Building) feels
                            burdensome and overly time-consuming.

                                                                                                                                                   LIGHT RAIL (CITY COLLEGE STATION)

                                                                                                                                                   CURTIS PARK PEDESTRIAN BRIDGE

                                                                                                                                                   PRIMARY CIRCULATION

                                                                                                                                                   SECONDARY CIRCULATION




                                                                                                                                                                                                     SCC MASTER PLAN
                                                                                                                                                   OPEN SPACE



                                                                                                                                                   KEY INTERSECTION




                                                                                                                                                                                                     HMC ARCHITECTS
                                        Curtis Park Pedestrian Bridge:
                                        A dedicated pedestrian bridge connects the Curtis Park neighborhood to the campus,
                                        spanning over the Sacramento Regional Transit Light Rail and Union Pacific Railroad tracks.




                                   SCC has the highest proportion of students walking to
                                   campus among the Los Rios colleges1.


                                    Pedestrian Circulation

                                    •       The northeast side of campus serves as the main
                                            pedestrian gateway, offering the most direct and clearly
                                            defined access.

                                    •       However, pedestrian routes along the campus perimeter
                                            often intersect with driveways & parking lots, creating
                                            unsafe conditions and frequent conflicts with vehicular
                                            traffic.

                                    •       Entry points on other campus edges are limited, poorly
                                            marked, or uninviting, making it challenging for students
                                            arriving from surrounding neighborhoods or public
                                            transit.

                                    •       Once inside, pedestrian circulation is fairly distributed
                                            between major buildings. However lack of proper
                                            wayfinding is a concern.

                                    •       Students report a lack of shading and weather
                                            protection along key walking paths, making circulation
                                            uncomfortable during hot or rainy days.

          1
              walksacramento.org
                                                                                                                                      Existing Main Campus Pedestrian Circulation & Open Space Map

                                                                                                                                                                                                         27
Pedestrian Circulation & Wayfinding
MAIN CAMPUS




                                                                                             11.11.2025
              Open Spaces




                                                                                             SCC MASTER PLAN
              •   The main quad is the largest open space and the symbolic heart of
                  campus—but students see it as underutilized and uninviting.

              •   The Quad lacks sufficient seating, shading, and activation, resulting in
                  minimal daily use.

              •   There is strong potential for the Quad to become a vibrant social and
                  activity hub with targeted improvements.




                                                                                             HMC ARCHITECTS
              •   The science quad, located near the main science building, is more
                  frequently used by students.

              •   However, it feels disconnected from the campus core due to the physical
                  barrier created by the LRC building, which limits visibility and flow.

              •   Across campus, students note a lack of flexible-use spaces that support
                  both informal gathering and programmed events (e.g., pop-ups,
                  performances, study lounges).

              •   Key amenities like Wi-Fi access, power outlets, seating variety, and
                  landscaping are unevenly distributed or missing in open areas,
                  diminishing the usability of outdoor spaces.

              •   Well-designed pedestrian circulation and open spaces are key to a
                  safe, welcoming, and connected campus. Clear walkways support easy
                  movement, while inviting open spaces encourage community, social
                  interaction, and student well-being.

                                                                                                                                                                            29
Campus Gateways
MAIN CAMPUS




                                                                                                                          11.11.2025
                                                                                                                          SCC MASTER PLAN
                                                                                                                          HMC ARCHITECTS
          The campus is welcoming to pedestrians approaching from Freeport Boulevard and 12th Avenue on the north
          side, with good sidewalks and clear access.

          However, the south gateways are less inviting. Crossing busy Sutterville Road and navigating multiple vehicle
          entrances creates safety and comfort issues for pedestrians. Walkways from the South Lot are also poor,
          despite shuttle service.

          Access from the parking lot on the east side, near the LUC building, is especially problematic. This entry
          feels underwhelming and uninviting, failing to provide a positive first impression for students and
          visitors arriving at campus for the first time.

          The City College Light Rail Station gateway is disconnected from the campus core by Hughes Stadium,
          forcing an indirect route with limited wayfinding, making access confusing for transit users.

          Additionally, nearby neighborhoods like William Land Park lack sidewalks in some areas, further limiting
          pedestrian access.

  SCC Space Utilization Study
  BY STEELCASE




                                                                                                                                                                       11.11.2025
                 A space utilization study was conducted by the Applied Research and Consulting Team at Steelcase to understand the change in
                 student, faculty and staff study and work patterns and the implications on space.


                 GOALS:




                                                                                                                                                                       SCC MASTER PLAN
                 •      Understand, at a high-level, student perspectives and aspirations for the campus experience

                 •      Investigate and document changes in faculty and classified professional work patterns

                 •      Define a range of concept designs to accommodate both faculty and classified professionals

                 •      Develop a range of Scenarios for classrooms across a spectrum of utilization targets and levels of Student demand




                                                                                                                                                                       HMC ARCHITECTS
                 •      Provide input to the facilities master plan

                                                                                                                                                                                                                     34
Classroom Usage
SPACE UTILIZATION STUDY: OBSERVATION FINDINGS




                                                                                                                    11.11.2025
             KEY FINDINGS




                                                                                                                    SCC MASTER PLAN
             The Steelcase Space Utilization Study presented the following major findings:

             •   There has been a significant shift in teaching modalities between 2018 and 2024; on-ground
                 teaching has shifted from approximately 80% to roughly 50% and there does not appear to be
                 a catalyst to change the current levels modality levels.

             •   The number of lecture and lab classrooms has remained unchanged between 2019 and 2024.




                                                                                                                    HMC ARCHITECTS
             •   Excess classroom capacity is indicated for all classroom types regardless of combination of
                 course days considered:

                    •   Monday - Sunday average utilization by room type is 21.2% for lecture and 29.4% for
                        lab.

                    •   Monday-Thursday average utilization by room type is 36% for lecture and 49.2% for
                        lab.

                    •   Utilization levels for Friday, Saturday, and Sunday are all low for all classroom types -
                        Sunday 0%, Saturday 0.6% or less, and Friday 9.2% or less.

                    •   Peak utilization of all classroom types tends to occur between the hours of 9:00 AM
                        and 2:00 PM.

             •   SCC Leader response to ideal long-term modality mix varied; one team was unable to come to
                 consensus and the other team results were on-ground 70% and online 30% which is different
                 than the Fall 2024 Weekly Enrollment Census statistics report where section data indicates
                 on-ground 50.7% and online 49.3%.

             •   Student success by modality generally indicates that overall, on-ground has higher success
                 than online, however partially online: under 50% (hybrid) has equal or higher success rates
                 than in-person in 2 of the 3 groups.

             •   Scenario and demand modeling indicate excess capacity in classrooms exist. It would be a
                 potential target for further investigation and implementation.

10.22.2025   SCC MASTER PLAN   HMC ARCHITECTS
                                                FRAMEWORK
Overview




                                                                                                                                                      11.11.2025
       This section outlines the methods used to determine how much space—and what types—are needed to support instructional programs and
       student services through the 2030 Master Plan horizon. It includes the following subsections:

       •   Enrollment Forecasts




                                                                                                                                                      SCC MASTER PLAN
       •   Calculating Space Needs

       •   Space Inventory Analysis

       •   Facilities Master Plan Space Programs




                                                                                                                                                      HMC ARCHITECTS
Enrollment Forecast

       The Long-Range Enrollment and Weekly Student Contact Hours (WSCH) forecasts are published each year by the California Community
       Colleges Chancellor’s Office (CCCCO).

       These forecasts show expected enrollment growth over the next 10 years, using past data and an average growth rate to estimate future
       enrollment and WSCH for the District. For this analysis, Fall 2024 was used as the base year, and projections extend to Fall 2027.




       SCC LONG RANGE ENROLLMENT FORECAST                                      SCC LONG RANGE WSCH FORECAST

                                                                  23,000                                                                    175,000

Calculating Space Needs                                                                                                           Space Inventory Analysis




                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         11.11.2025
                                                                                                                                  CAPACITY LOAD RATIO




      To determine how much space a college needs, enrollment and program forecasts are matched                                               Capacity/load ratios show the relationship between the amount of space available (by
      with space standards set by Title 5 of the California Code of Regulations1. These rules guide how                                       type) and the number of students using that space. Some space types, listed as “other,”
      educational spaces should be planned and used on community college campuses. By applying these                                          are not included in CCCCO’s capacity/load analysis—these may include maintenance and




                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         SCC MASTER PLAN
      standards to student numbers or Weekly Student Contact Hours (WSCH), we can calculate how                                               operations areas that are important to the college's operations.
      much space is needed, measured in assignable square feet (ASF).
                                                                                                                                              The capacity/load ratio measures how efficiently space is being used based on Title 5
      ASF refers to the parts of a building that can be used by people—like classrooms, offices, and labs.                                    standards.
      Gross square feet (GSF) includes everything inside the building’s exterior walls, including hallways,
      restrooms, and mechanical areas.                                                                                                          •   For classrooms, the expected use is 53 hours per week; lab use varies depending on
                                                                                                                                                    the program.




                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         HMC ARCHITECTS
      The table to the right lists the Title 5 standards used in this calculation. Each type of space is
      matched with the right kind of enrollment data to estimate how much ASF is needed. Adding all the                                         •   Ratios are calculated by space type and then totaled for each campus.
      space types together gives the total space requirement for the College.

10.22.2025   SCC MASTER PLAN   HMC ARCHITECTS
                                                RECOMMENDATIONS
Overview




                                                         11.11.2025
      The 2025 Facilities Master Plan (FMP)
      recommendations for the Sacramento City
      College campus integrate the condition and
      space utilization trends of existing facilities,




                                                         SCC MASTER PLAN
      enrollment projections, and the campus
      community’s long-term vision for the
      future of development on the campus. The
      recommendations address the campus’s
      current and future needs and are intended
      to be carried out in several phases, with
      each phase building on the work that was
      conducted in prior phases. The FMP plans




                                                         HMC ARCHITECTS
      and diagrams appear to be specific but
      are intended to allow future design teams,
      working in conjunction with the campus
      community, to identify and address issues
      and concerns specific to each project at the
      time it is carried out.

      Recommendations for the future
      development of the campus are presented
      in the following sections:


         •   Planning Principals

         •   Facilities Master Plan

         •   Vehicular Circulation

         •   Pedestrian Circulation

         •   Campus Identity

         •   Wayfinding

         •   Proposed Projects

         •   Sustainability

         •   Security and Safety

         •   Phased Development




                                                             43
Planning Principles




                                                                                                                                                             11.11.2025
       The Facilities Master Plan is grounded by a set of Planning Principals that emerged through extensive discussions with the Planning Committee,
       college staff, and other campus stakeholders. The Principals express the driving ethos of the FMP and articulate the values and aspirations for
       the physical infrastructure of the campus. Each of the five Principals inform the recommendations in the FMP and serve as a guide to evaluating
       various planning options; and, they are intended as a framework to inform and evaluate each subsequent phase, and each project, in the FMP as it is




                                                                                                                                                             SCC MASTER PLAN
       implemented over time.

       Facilities Planning Principles


                                                                            •   Upgrading technology




                                                                                                                                                             HMC ARCHITECTS
                                                                            •   Address aging facilities

                                                  Student Success           •   Providing student collaboration spaces

                                                                            •   Develop campus to keep students on campus

                                                                            •   Learning Resource Center and Maker Space




              Campus & Community                                                        Effective & Efficient

              •   Improve outdoor spaces to encourage collaboration                     •   Improve space utilization

              •   Improve cafeteria spaces to provide additional food                   •   Revisit use for LRC building
                  options
                                                                                        •   Instructional spaces for CTE programs, such as aviation.
              •   Integration of public art on campus
                                                                                        •   Flexible spaces designed for a range of uses
              •   Enhance sense of belonging and pride
                                                                                        •   Maximize funding sources
              •   Enhance campus security and safety
                                                                                        •   Renovate buildings for well-being
              •   Promote human connection and interaction



              Stewardship of Resources                                                  Access

              •   People first: Exercise stewardship of campus community                •   Improve campus street presence

              •   Preserve and enhance key campus facilities                            •   Creating welcoming campus gateways for student access

              •   Prioritize long-term investments                                      •   Improving access to programs and services

              •   Protect natural resources                                             •   Enhance wayfinding

                                                  subjects and modalities of teaching,
                                                  and flexible to allow easy upgrades of                     Campus Gateways will create a sense of arrival for campus users and will enhance the
                                                  critical equipment over time.                              identity of the SCC as a unique and important place. As part of a complete wayfinding
                                                                                                             strategy, gateways will aid pedestrian wayfinding and will help to orient campus
                                                                                                             visitors. Iconic by definition, the addition of gateways to Sacramento City College’s
                                                                                                             campus is an opportunity to create memorable structures whose design exemplifies
                                                                                                             the spirt of the campus.

                                              Lot, and in faculty and staff parking areas.

Pedestrian Circulation

              The major components of pedestrian movement consist of movement from parking to program areas;                        IMPROVED/ ADDED
              circulation from transit stops to program areas; circulation between program areas; and, movement of                  PEDESTRIAN & GREEN SPACE

                                                                                                                                                                              SCC MASTER PLAN

              to Sutterville Road, circulation improvements at the West Lot, and wayfinding improvements, to name a

              pedestrian circulation on the campus.

               New Science Building Quad vs. Main Quad

              Mohr Hall and the Quad. This will become especially important when the new academic building adjacent to
              the Mohr Hall is completed.

Campus Identity & Wayfinding
MAIN CAMPUS




                                                                                                                                11.11.2025
          Sacramento City College has a long history as a center for learning and community in Sacramento. Strengthening
          the campus identity along Freeport Boulevard and Sutterville Road will make the campus more visible, welcoming,
          and connected to the neighborhood.




                                                                                                                                SCC MASTER PLAN
          External
          External Identity
                     Identity

           Frontages along Freeport and Sutterville should signal that this is a vibrant, active campus. Enhancements could
          include improved wayfinding, landscaping, signage, and building treatments that highlight signature structures
          like Rodda Hall, Mohr Hall, and the Natural Sciences Building, creating a strong first impression for visitors and
          passersby.




                                                                                                                                HMC ARCHITECTS
          Internal
          Internal Identity
                     Identity

          Within the campus, pedestrian pathways, open spaces, and gathering areas should encourage interaction and
          engagement. Clear circulation from the street edges into the quad and between key buildings will help make the
          campus feel more approachable, connected, and centered on student activity.

              Wayfinding

          The projects described in this FMP, when completed, will organize campus programs to increase the efficiency
          of space utilization as well as the movement of people and vehicles on the campus. The opportunity exists to
          maximize the impact and efficacy of those changes by developing a comprehensive, campus-wide wayfinding
          strategy that incorporates the future state of the campus.

          Primary issues that should be considered while developing a wayfinding package include:

                •   Install Clear Directional Signs                         •   Integrate Technology
                    Place easy-to-read signs at key points to guide             Use digital wayfinding tools, such as
                    pedestrians and drivers.                                    interactive kiosks and mobile apps, for real-
                                                                                time navigation.
                •   Provide Campus Maps
                    Position detailed maps at major entrances, parking      •   Maintain Aesthetic Consistency
                    areas, and high-traffic locations.                          Ensure all signage reflects the college’s
                                                                                branding and aesthetic.
                •   Mark Buildings Clearly
                    Label all buildings with visible and consistent         •   Enhance Safety
                    signage for easy identification.                            Include emergency information and
                                                                                evacuation routes in the signage system.
                •   Ensure Accessibility
                    Design all signage to be ADA-compliant, including
                    braille and other accessible features.

          These actions will create a more effective, user-friendly, and inclusive wayfinding system, enhancing the overall
          campus experience.

                                                                                                                                    56
Sustainability




                                                                                                                                                                                                11.11.2025
       Environmental sustainability has been a central component of California Community College’s planning
       efforts since 2013, when the California Community College Board of Governors released the first system-wide
       sustainability plan. That plan evolved and in 2019 the Climate Change and Sustainability Policy was adopted by
       the California Community College Board of Governors. The 2019 policy aligned with state policy goals and efforts




                                                                                                                                                                                                SCC MASTER PLAN
       that detail statewide energy conservation, greenhouse gas reduction, de-carbonization, sustainability, and
       climate change mitigation measures.

       The 2019 Climate Change and Sustainability Policy has since been revised into a framework that establishes
       systemwide goals and targets, and integrates a comprehensive approach to leverage both colleges’ physical and
       social impact footprint. This framework, titled the Climate Action and Sustainability Framework (“Framework”),
       is intended to,




                                                                                                                                                                                                HMC ARCHITECTS
                 “inspire and empower institutions to act on bold climate commitments and to create innovative
                 climate solutions. It also recognizes district-level progress is achieved at different stages. As such,
                 the Framework first asks California’s community colleges to establish benchmarks. Next, the Climate
                 Action and Sustainability Framework asks districts and colleges to track progress towards the goals
                 for 2025, 2030, and ultimately 2035, the target year for the state of California to reduce greenhouse
                 gas emission by 100% below the baseline.” 1



       The Framework provides metric-based sustainability goals across eight areas of activity as listed below. Each
       Community College District is responsible, with support from the Chancellor’s Office, for implementing
       measures to meet these goals on a District-wide basis.


                              1.    Greenhouse Gas Emission Reduction                                         5. Purchasing & Procurement

                              2. Green Buildings                                                              6. Waste

                              3. Energy                                                                       7.    Transportation

                              4. Water                                                                        8. Food Systems


       Not all of these areas of emphasis are within the purview of this Facilities Master Plan, which is focused on the
       physical infrastructure of Sacramento Community College. Purchasing and Procurement, and Food Systems and
       managed by the District separately and are distinct from campus facilities management activities.




       1   California Community College Chancellor’s Office. “California Community Colleges Board of Governors Climate Action and Sustainability Framework”. 2025. Online: https://www.cccco.
           edu/-/media/CCCCO-Website/docs/data-resources/californiacommunitycollegesboardofgovernorsclimateactionandsustainabilityframeworka11y-1.pdf. Retrieved August 1, 2025.



                                                                                                                                                                                                    57
Sustainability




                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                11.11.2025
       While the Framework encourages Districts and Colleges to meet specific sustainability metrics, discretion is given to those entities to identify the optimal approach to sustainable design based on their portfolio of projects,
       budget, and by taking local considerations into account. Sustainability goals adopted for the Los Rios Community College District and Sacramento City College include:




                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                SCC MASTER PLAN

                                                               and Maintenance (O&M) Silver or
                                                               WELL Silver equivalent.


       Implementation

       As described in this plan, Sacramento City College has limited need for new buildings. As such, the most significant opportunities to advance Campus sustainability will likely be found in the environmental optimization of existing
       buildings, landscaping, and site infrastructure. Design teams working on building modernizations, system upgrades, and site improvements should use the goals described above as a baseline for environmental performance
       and should seek to improve on these metrics where circumstances allow. The creation of EUI and carbon emissions baselines is an important first step in sustainable facility management, and these baselines can be periodically
       revisited to assess progress. When new facilities are planned, design teams are encouraged to pursue building designs that exceed the recommendations noted above and that emphasize energy and water efficiency, elimination
       of greenhouse gas emissions, and that contribute to high levels of occupant health and wellness.

                  14. Attendees noted that the campus has a shortage of spaces to host large groups and/or large events,

                                                                                                                                                     dŚĞƌĞŝƐĂĚĞƐŝƌĞƚŽƚƵƌŶƚŚĞďƵŝůĚŝŶŐŝŶƚŽdͲĨŽĐƵƐĞĚďƵŝůĚŝŶŐ͘
                                                                                                                                         3.     ADA concerns have impacted access to parts of the building—particularly for large equipment.
                  
                                                                                                                                         4.     Restroom limitations: the building converted from multiple stalls to single-user restrooms, which
                                                                                                                                                 is insufficient for current needs.
                  
                                                                                                                                         5.     The HVAC program (established in 2001) currently occupies most of the building. 
                                                                                                                                                a. The HVAC program will remain in place, requiring building upgrades to support it.
                  
                                                                                                                                                 b. The building has self-maintained chillers and boilers, raising questions about maintenance
                  
                                                                                                                                                    responsibilities.
                  
                                                                                                                                                c.    Flexible power infrastructure is needed to support evolving lab requirements and program
                                                                                                                                                       changes. Suggest looking into flexible lab models like the Davis Center, including floor outlets. 
                  
                                                                                                                                                d. There is a transformer located adjacent to the building currently serves the facility, pulling
                                                                                                                                                   additional power would be doable.
                                                                                                                                         6.     A former roll-up door up the ramp in the loading dock area was replaced with a window; this must
                                                                                                                                                be restored for large equipment access while meeting ADA standards.
                                                                                                                                                 a. There is a desire to repurpose the loading dock and add additional roll-up doors to facilitate
                                                                                                                                                    program expansion into the former bookstore space.
                                                                                                                                          7.     Large lab spaces are in good condition structurally but need cosmetic refreshes and smart
                                                                                                                                                 infrastructure upgrades (e.g., painting, modern utilities).

                                                                                                                                                         e. Interiors need refreshing and tech upgrades; windows are a highlight.
                                                                                                                                                    3.   Academic needs:

                                                                                                                                                         a. The center currently has no built-in labs; some classrooms converted to labs (esp.
                                                                                                                                                             chemistry). There has been debates on labs needs due to center’s proximity to main
                                                                                                                                                            campus.

                                                                                                                                                         b. There is a plan for possible lab sharing program with Washington School District (Fall
                                                                                                                                                             2027).
                      
                                                                                                                                                     4.   Enrollment/programming:
                                                                                                                                                         a. Centers must meet 1,000 FTEs annually (currently ~20% online).
                                                                                                                                                         b. West Sac Center currently serves healthcare, nearby middle and high school programs,
                                                                                                                                                             General Education programs, and evening ESL.
                      
                                                                                                                                                          c.   There has been a strong, growing demand for in-person academic service; scheduling
                                                                                                                                                              middle/ high school use in the morning and center programs in the evening to maximize
                                                                                                                                                               all-day activity
                      
                                                                                                                                                     5.   Student experience:
                      
                                                                                                                                                          a. The center needs more student meeting spaces that avoid “high school” aesthetic.
                                                                                                                                                          b. The center would benefit from the addition of covered, secured outdoor space, and the
                                                                                                                                                             redesign or extension of fencing boundary to create safe, usable outdoor/green areas. 
                                                                                                                                                          c.   There is currently a courtyard fence project proposal under discussion with Los Rios.

                                campus.

                                                                                                                                        HMC ARCHITECTS
                     6.    Community integration:
                           a. There has been a decline in student use of community center café post-pandemic.
                           b. The community center next door is heavily used by older citizen’s program.
                           c.   There is a potential synergy between middle/high school, college, and senior programs.
           2
                     7.    Funding challenges:
                           a. Interior/FFE upgrades are ineligible for state funding (not 50+ years old). There is an
                              opportunity to explore masterplan and bond-support options.
                     8.    Security concerns:
                           a. Security is an ongoing issue in West Sac location.
                           b. Enclosing area would improve safety and community feel.
                           c.   The center would benefit from a shift in focus from building-level fixes to campus-level
                                planning, potentially creating quad/shared art & education complex with community
                                center.
                                
                C. Action Items
                    
                   1. HMC to explore adding West Sac Center upgrades into the masterplan and securing bond
                       support.
                   2. Review fencing design and propose extended/enclosed boundary to improve security and create
                       outdoor space.
                   3. Advance discussion with Los Rios on adding a courtyard fence for the south side.
                   4. Explore partnership with Washington School District for shared labs (target: Fall 2027).
                   5. Evaluate lab needs in relation to proximity to main campus.
                   6. Plan interior and tech refresh for West Sac Center (student spaces, meeting rooms, classrooms).
                   7. Develop strategy to integrate middle college HS, adult career ed, and senior programs into
                       facilities use.
                   8. Assess opportunities to strengthen community ties (e.g., theater, community center café).
                   9. Shift planning lens from building-level to campus-level improvements for West Sac Center.

           4
                    1. The group agreed not to pursue Certificates of Participation (COPs) to avoid district debt.

                                                                                                                                                good condition; up to 10% is considered fair condition; over 10% is considered poor
                                                                                                                                                condition; and, over 100% indicates more cost effective to replace building than to
                                                                                                                                                modernize.

                                                                                                                        dŚĞĂďŽǀĞŶŽƚĞƐĚŽĐƵŵĞŶƚŽƵƌƵŶĚĞƌƐƚĂŶĚŝŶŐŽĨŝƚĞŵƐĚŝƐĐƵƐƐĞĚŝŶƚŚĞĂďŽǀĞͲƌĞĨĞƌĞŶĐĞĚŵĞĞƚŝŶŐ͘hŶůĞƐƐ
                      c. Rodda Hall North and Rodda Hall South are more competitive for state funding than HSP.
                                                                                                                        ŶŽƚŝĐĞƚŽƚŚĞĐŽŶƚƌĂƌǇŝƐƌĞĐĞŝǀĞĚ͕ƚŚĞŶŽƚĂƚŝŽŶƐǁŝůůďĞĐŽŶƐŝĚĞƌĞĚĂĐĐĞƉƚĂďůĞĂŶĚĂĐĐƵƌĂƚĞĂŶĚ,Dǁŝůů
                      d. The Hoos Pool renovation will address MEP and Department of Health requirements as a           ƉƌŽĐĞĞĚǁŝƚŚǁŽƌŬďĂƐĞĚŽŶƚŚĞƐĞƵŶĚĞƌƐƚĂŶĚŝŶŐƐ͘ŶǇĚŝƐĐƌĞƉĂŶĐŝĞƐƐŚŽƵůĚďĞďƌŽƵŐŚƚƚŽŽƵƌĂƚƚĞŶƚŝŽŶ
                         systems, maintenance, and repair project, without adding square footage.                      ǁŝƚŚŝŶƚŚƌĞĞ;ϯͿǁŽƌŬŝŶŐĚĂǇƐŽĨƌĞĐĞŝƉƚ͘
                      e. The Business (BUS) building program is currently mostly online, and classrooms are used
                         by other programs, so there may not be a need to add more swing space to                       
                         accommodate for the business program during construction.
                  2. Locally funded projects will include LRC, CST, COS, MUSIC, and LUC renovations.
                      a. LRC modernization will remain interior only, without exterior modernization, as it is not
                         a strong candidate for state funding 
                            i. Opportunity for other programs to move into the LRC, which is currently underused
                               and does not need all its space.
                            ii. The library trend is shifting toward becoming student service spaces, and the LRC
                                can support that change.
                           iii. The LRC project can be phased over several years during summers and will be
                                locally funded.
                      b. Removing CIC remains a wish list item but must be vetted against bond funding levels
                         and district-wide projects.
                      c. The campus gateway should move up in priority, while Freeport should move to the end.
                      d. Quad improvements can be completed piecemeal, some through FF&E, with an eye for
                         enhancement opportunities.
                  3. A large lecture room is not included in the scope because there is no programmatic
                     justification, with only 10–12 events per year. It will be difficult to secure state funding for
                     any new square footage additions to campus.
                      
               E. Next Steps
                  1. HMC to present the phasing proposal to the larger planning committee in the September
                     26th meeting.

                                                                                                                                             the plan adaptable to future needs.

                        
              2D. LRC and Business Building Planning
                   1.   The LRC was discussed as a future campus hub, providing swing space for tutoring, assessment,
                        student support, and a consolidated media center.
                   2.   The new business building is expected to house instructional space, a café, and possibly a
                        makerspace while maintaining a net-zero square footage balance.
                             a. More detailed programming for the building will take place once projects are approved,
                                working directly with building users to determine specific needs.
                             b. The team will develop plans to transform the LRC into a campus hub, with the option to
                                relocate makerspace to either LRC or the new business building, and determine future uses
                                for the current makerspace building
               
               E. Next Steps
                   F. The committee recognized the importance of documenting existing conditions and maintaining
                      consistent architectural vocabulary within the master plan.
                   1.   Draft of the masterplan to be presented at the October board retreat, with final presentation
                        scheduled for December. 
                             a. HMC to assist in creating the presentation deck
                             b. The committee agreed that community engagement prior to board approval is crucial and
                                proposed a large campus meeting to gather input before the December presentation
                   2.   The team will determine project sequencing to minimize campus impact and evaluate state funding
                        opportunities.
                             a. The district to coordinate on the phasing schedule and plans.
                             b. Swing space locations for phased projects to be identified.
